</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic_role">Symbolic role</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2076" data-file-width="1384" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/170px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/255px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG/340px-6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:6852_les_deniers_de_judas.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century fresco painting of Judas being paid thirty pieces of silver for his betrayal of Jesus</div></div></div>
<p>Silver plays a certain role in mythology and has found various usage as a metaphor and in folklore. The Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Works_and_Days" title="Works and Days">Works and Days</a></i> (lines 109–201) lists different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ages_of_man" title="Ages of man">ages of man</a> named after metals like gold, silver, bronze and iron to account for successive ages of humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i> contains another retelling of the story, containing an illustration of silver's metaphorical use of signifying the second-best in a series, better than bronze but worse than gold:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>But when good <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a>, banish'd from above,<br/>
Was driv'n to Hell, the world was under <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jove</a>.<br/>
Succeeding times a silver age behold,<br/>
Excelling brass, but more excell'd by gold.
</p>
</div><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Ovid, <a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a>, Book I, trans. <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In folklore, silver was commonly thought to have mystic powers: for example, a <a href="/wiki/Silver_bullet" title="Silver bullet">bullet cast from silver</a> is often supposed in such folklore the only weapon that is effective against a <a href="/wiki/Werewolf" title="Werewolf">werewolf</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witch" title="Witch">witch</a>, or other <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monsters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-narodna_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-narodna-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StClair_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-StClair-72">[72]</a></sup> From this the idiom of a <a href="/wiki/Silver_bullet" title="Silver bullet">silver bullet</a> developed into figuratively referring to any simple solution with very high effectiveness or almost miraculous results, as in the widely discussed <a href="/wiki/Software_engineering" title="Software engineering">software engineering</a> paper <i><a href="/wiki/No_Silver_Bullet" title="No Silver Bullet">No Silver Bullet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Other powers attributed to silver include detection of poison and facilitation of passage into the <a href="/wiki/Fairyland" title="Fairyland">mythical realm of fairies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StClair_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-StClair-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver production has also inspired figurative language. Clear references to cupellation occur throughout the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, such as in <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>'s rebuke to Judah: "The bellows are burned, the lead is consumed of the fire; the founder melteth in vain: for the wicked are not plucked away. Reprobate silver shall men call them, because the Lord hath rejected them." (Jeremiah 6:19–20) Jeremiah was also aware of sheet silver, exemplifying the malleability and ductility of the metal: "Silver spread into plates is brought from Tarshish, and gold from Uphaz, the work of the workman, and of the hands of the founder: blue and purple is their clothing: they are all the work of cunning men." (Jeremiah 10:9)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks14_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks14-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver also has more negative cultural meanings: the idiom <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thirty_pieces_of_silver" title="wikt:thirty pieces of silver">thirty pieces of silver</a>, referring to a reward for betrayal, references the bribe <a href="/wiki/Judas_Iscariot" title="Judas Iscariot">Judas Iscariot</a> is said in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> to have taken from Jewish leaders in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> to turn <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus of Nazareth</a> over to soldiers of the high priest Caiaphas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Ethically, silver also symbolizes greed and degradation of consciousness; this is the negative aspect, the perverting of its value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Silver_mining" title="Silver mining">Silver mining</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine,_Jbel_Saghro,_Tinghir,_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet,_Morocco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4200" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg/220px-Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg/330px-Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg/440px-Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine%2C_Jbel_Saghro%2C_Tinghir%2C_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet%2C_Morocco.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acanthite_-_Imiter_mine,_Jbel_Saghro,_Tinghir,_Dr%C3%A2a-Tafilalet,_Morocco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acanthite sample from the <a href="/wiki/Imider" title="Imider">Imider</a> mine in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a></div></div></div>
<p>The abundance of silver in the Earth's crust is 0.08 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a>, almost exactly the same as that of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>. It mostly occurs in <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> ores, especially <a href="/wiki/Acanthite" title="Acanthite">acanthite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentite" title="Argentite">argentite</a>, Ag<sub>2</sub>S. Argentite deposits sometimes also contain <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native</a> silver when they occur in reducing environments, and when in contact with salt water they are converted to <a href="/wiki/Chlorargyrite" title="Chlorargyrite">chlorargyrite</a> (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horn_silver" title="Horn silver">horn silver</a>), AgCl, which is prevalent in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1174_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1174-76">[76]</a></sup> Most other silver minerals are silver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pnictide" title="Pnictide">pnictides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chalcogenide" title="Chalcogenide">chalcogenides</a>; they are generally lustrous semiconductors. Most true silver deposits, as opposed to argentiferous deposits of other metals, came from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tertiary_period" title="Tertiary period">Tertiary period</a> vulcanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann21_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann21-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The principal sources of silver are the ores of copper, copper-nickel, lead, and lead-zinc obtained from <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-9">[9]</a></sup> Peru, Bolivia and Mexico have been mining silver since 1546, and are still major world producers. Top silver-producing mines are <a href="/wiki/Cannington_Mine" title="Cannington Mine">Cannington</a> (Australia), <a href="/wiki/Mina_Proa%C3%B1o" title="Mina Proaño">Fresnillo</a> (Mexico), <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_mine_(Bolivia)" title="San Cristóbal mine (Bolivia)">San Cristóbal</a> (Bolivia), <a href="/wiki/Antamina_mine" title="Antamina mine">Antamina</a> (Peru), <a href="/wiki/Rudna_mine" title="Rudna mine">Rudna</a> (Poland), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pe%C3%B1asquito_Polymetallic_Mine" title="Peñasquito Polymetallic Mine">Penasquito</a> (Mexico).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPM_Group_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPM_Group-78">[78]</a></sup> Top near-term mine development projects through 2015 are Pascua Lama (Chile), Navidad (Argentina), Jaunicipio (Mexico), Malku Khota (Bolivia),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and Hackett River (Canada).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPM_Group_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-CPM_Group-78">[78]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mining_in_Tajikistan#Silver" title="Mining in Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> is known to have some of the largest silver deposits in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silver is usually found in nature combined with other metals, or in minerals that contain silver compounds, generally in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfides" title="Sulfides">sulfides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a> (lead sulfide) or <a href="/wiki/Cerussite" title="Cerussite">cerussite</a> (lead carbonate). So the primary production of silver requires the smelting and then <a href="/wiki/Cupellation" title="Cupellation">cupellation</a> of argentiferous lead ores, a historically important process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kassianidou_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kassianidou-81">[81]</a></sup> Lead melts at 327 °C, lead oxide at 888 °C and silver melts at 960 °C. To separate the silver, the alloy is melted again at the high temperature of 960 °C to 1000 °C in an oxidizing environment. The lead oxidises to <a href="/wiki/Lead(II)_oxide" title="Lead(II) oxide">lead monoxide</a>, then known as <a href="/wiki/Litharge" title="Litharge">litharge</a>, which captures the oxygen from the other metals present. The liquid lead oxide is removed or absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">capillary action</a> into the hearth linings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BayleyCrossleyPonting_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-BayleyCrossleyPonting-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Ag</span>(s) + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">Pb</span>(s) + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>(g) → 2<span class="chemf nowrap">PbO</span>(absorbed) + Ag(l)</dd></dl>
<p>Today, silver metal is primarily produced instead as a secondary byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Refining_(metallurgy)#Electrolytic_refining" title="Refining (metallurgy)">electrolytic refining</a> of copper, lead, and zinc, and by application of the <a href="/wiki/Parkes_process" title="Parkes process">Parkes process</a> on lead bullion from ore that also contains silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-85">[85]</a></sup> In such processes, silver follows the non-ferrous metal in question through its concentration and smelting, and is later purified out. For example, in copper production, purified copper is <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolytically</a> deposited on the cathode, while the less reactive precious metals such as silver and gold collect under the anode as the so-called "anode slime". This is then separated and purified of base metals by treatment with hot aerated dilute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfuric" title="Sulfuric">sulfuric</a> acid and heating with lime or silica flux, before the silver is purified to over 99.9% purity via electrolysis in <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1174_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1174-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial-grade fine silver is at least 99.9% pure, and purities greater than 99.999% are available. In 2014, Mexico was the top producer of silver (5,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> or 18.7% of the world's total of 26,800 t), followed by China (4,060 t) and Peru (3,780 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_marine_environments">In marine environments</span></h3>
<p>Silver concentration is low in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> (pmol/L). Levels vary by depth and between water bodies. Dissolved silver concentrations range from 0.3 pmol/L in coastal surface waters to 22.8 pmol/L in pelagic deep waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarriadaTappin2007_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarriadaTappin2007-86">[86]</a></sup> Analyzing the presence and dynamics of silver in marine environments is difficult due to these particularly low concentrations and complex interactions in the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FischerSmith2018_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FischerSmith2018-87">[87]</a></sup> Although a rare trace metal, concentrations are greatly impacted by fluvial, aeolian, atmospheric, and upwelling inputs, as well as anthropogenic inputs via discharge, waste disposal, and emissions from industrial companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ndung’uThomas2001_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ndung’uThomas2001-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZhangAmakawa2001_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZhangAmakawa2001-89">[89]</a></sup> Other internal processes such as decomposition of organic matter may be a source of dissolved silver in deeper waters, which feeds into some surface waters through upwelling and vertical mixing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZhangAmakawa2001_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZhangAmakawa2001-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Atlantic and Pacific, silver concentrations are minimal at the surface but rise in deeper waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlegalSañudo-Wilhelmy1995_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FlegalSañudo-Wilhelmy1995-90">[90]</a></sup> Silver is taken up by plankton in the photic zone, remobilized with depth, and enriched in deep waters. Silver is transported from the Atlantic to the other oceanic water masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ndung’uThomas2001_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ndung’uThomas2001-88">[88]</a></sup> In North Pacific waters, silver is remobilized at a slower rate and increasingly enriched compared to deep Atlantic waters. Silver has increasing concentrations that follow the major oceanic conveyor belt that cycles water and nutrients from the North Atlantic to the South Atlantic to the North Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RanvilleFlegal2005_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-RanvilleFlegal2005-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is not an extensive amount of data focused on how marine life is affected by silver despite the likely deleterious effects it could have on organisms through <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a>, association with particulate matters, and <a href="/wiki/Sorption" title="Sorption">sorption</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarriadaTappin2007_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-BarriadaTappin2007-86">[86]</a></sup> Not until about 1984 did scientists begin to understand the chemical characteristics of silver and the potential toxicity. In fact, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> is the only other trace metal that surpasses the toxic effects of silver; however, the full extent of silver toxicity is not expected in oceanic conditions because of its ability to transfer into nonreactive biological compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratte1999_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ratte1999-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one study, the presence of excess ionic silver and silver nanoparticles caused bioaccumulation effects on zebrafish organs and altered the chemical pathways within their gills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LacaveVicario-Parés2018_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-LacaveVicario-Parés2018-93">[93]</a></sup> In addition, very early experimental studies demonstrated how the toxic effects of silver fluctuate with salinity and other parameters, as well as between life stages and different species such as finfish, molluscs, and crustaceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Another study found raised concentrations of silver in the muscles and liver of dolphins and whales, indicating pollution of this metal within recent decades. Silver is not an easy metal for an organism to eliminate and elevated concentrations can cause death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChenZhuang2017_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChenZhuang2017-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_use"><span class="anchor" id="XAG"></span>Monetary use</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:American_Silver_Eagle,_obverse,_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="487" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg/220px-American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg/330px-American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg/440px-American_Silver_Eagle%2C_obverse%2C_2004.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:American_Silver_Eagle,_obverse,_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 2004 <a href="/wiki/American_Silver_Eagle" title="American Silver Eagle">American Silver Eagle</a> bullion coin, minted in .999 fine silver.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known coins were minted in the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> around 600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coins_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-coins-96">[96]</a></sup> The coins of Lydia were made of <a href="/wiki/Electrum" title="Electrum">electrum</a>, which is a naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> of gold and silver, that was available within the territory of Lydia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coins_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-coins-96">[96]</a></sup> Since that time, <a href="/wiki/Silver_standard" title="Silver standard">silver standards</a>, in which the standard economic <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_account" title="Unit of account">unit of account</a> is a fixed weight of silver, have been widespread throughout the world until the 20th century. Notable <a href="/wiki/Silver_coin" title="Silver coin">silver coins</a> through the centuries include the <a href="/wiki/Greek_drachma" title="Greek drachma">Greek drachma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> the Roman <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Dirham" title="Dirham">dirham</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oeddirhem_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-oeddirhem-99">[99]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Karshapana" title="Karshapana">karshapana</a> from ancient India and <a href="/wiki/Rupee" title="Rupee">rupee</a> from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> (grouped with copper and gold coins to create a trimetallic standard),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">Spanish dollar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodcock2009_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodcock2009-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne2012_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne2012-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ratio between the amount of silver used for coinage and that used for other purposes has fluctuated greatly over time; for example, in wartime, more silver tends to have been used for coinage to finance the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann63_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann63-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, silver bullion has the <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">ISO 4217</a> currency code XAG, one of only four <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> to have one (the others being <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, and gold).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Silver coins are produced from cast rods or ingots, rolled to the correct thickness, heat-treated, and then used to cut <a href="/wiki/Planchet" title="Planchet">blanks</a> from. These blanks are then milled and minted in a coining press; modern coining presses can produce 8000 silver coins per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann63_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann63-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Price_of_silver.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1425" data-file-width="5439" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Price_of_silver.webp/300px-Price_of_silver.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Price_of_silver.webp/450px-Price_of_silver.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Price_of_silver.webp/600px-Price_of_silver.webp.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Price_of_silver.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Price of silver 1968-2022</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Silver_as_an_investment" title="Silver as an investment">silver as an investment</a></div>
<p>Silver prices are normally quoted in <a href="/wiki/Troy_weight" title="Troy weight">troy ounces</a>. One troy ounce is equal to 31.1034768 grams. The London silver fix is published every working day at noon London time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> This price is determined by several major international banks and is used by <a href="/wiki/London_bullion_market" title="London bullion market">London bullion market</a> members for trading that day. Prices are most commonly shown as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollar</a> (USD), the <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">Pound sterling</a> (GBP), and the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">Euro</a> (EUR).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewellery_and_silverware">Jewellery and silverware</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg/220px-Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg/330px-Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg/440px-Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rennen_Silver_sarcophagus_of_Saint_Stanislaus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9_and_chasing" title="Repoussé and chasing">Embossed</a> silver sarcophagus of <a href="/wiki/Stanislaus_of_Szczepan%C3%B3w" title="Stanislaus of Szczepanów">Saint Stanislaus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Wawel_Cathedral" title="Wawel Cathedral">Wawel Cathedral</a> was created in main centers of the 17th century European <a href="/wiki/Silversmith" title="Silversmith">silversmithery</a> - <a href="/wiki/Augsburg" title="Augsburg">Augsburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gdańsk">Gdańsk</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artinpl_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-artinpl-106">[106]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg/220px-L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg/330px-L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg/440px-L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%C3%A4ck%C3%B6_slott_interior_42.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>17th century silverware</div></div></div>
<p>The major use of silver besides coinage throughout most of history was in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Jewellery" title="Jewellery">jewellery</a> and other general-use items, and this continues to be a major use today. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Household_silver" title="Household silver">table silver</a> for cutlery, for which silver is highly suited due to its antibacterial properties. <a href="/wiki/Western_concert_flute" title="Western concert flute">Western concert flutes</a> are usually plated with or made out of <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann65_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann65-107">[107]</a></sup> in fact, most silverware is only silver-plated rather than made out of pure silver; the silver is normally put in place by <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a>. Silver-plated glass (as opposed to metal) is used for mirrors, <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_flask" title="Vacuum flask">vacuum flasks</a>, and Christmas tree decorations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann67_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann67-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because pure silver is very soft, most silver used for these purposes is alloyed with copper, with finenesses of 925/1000, 835/1000, and 800/1000 being common. One drawback is the easy tarnishing of silver in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and its derivatives. Including precious metals such as palladium, platinum, and gold gives resistance to tarnishing but is quite costly; <a href="/wiki/Base_metal" title="Base metal">base metals</a> like <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a> do not totally prevent corrosion and tend to affect the lustre and colour of the alloy. Electrolytically refined pure silver plating is effective at increasing resistance to tarnishing. The usual solutions for restoring the lustre of tarnished silver are dipping baths that reduce the silver sulfide surface to metallic silver, and cleaning off the layer of tarnish with a paste; the latter approach also has the welcome side effect of polishing the silver concurrently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann65_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann65-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medical_uses_of_silver" title="Medical uses of silver">Medical uses of silver</a></div>
<p>In medicine, silver is incorporated into wound dressings and used as an antibiotic coating in medical devices. Wound dressings containing <a href="/wiki/Silver_sulfadiazine" title="Silver sulfadiazine">silver sulfadiazine</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_nanoparticles" title="Silver nanoparticles">silver nanomaterials</a> are used to treat external infections. Silver is also used in some medical applications, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinary_catheter" title="Urinary catheter">urinary catheters</a> (where tentative evidence indicates it reduces catheter-related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinary_tract_infections" title="Urinary tract infections">urinary tract infections</a>) and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endotracheal_tube" title="Endotracheal tube">endotracheal breathing tubes</a> (where evidence suggests it reduces ventilator-associated <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bou2012_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bou2012-110">[110]</a></sup> The silver <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a> is <a href="/wiki/Biological_activity" title="Biological activity">bioactive</a> and in sufficient <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentration</a> readily kills <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i>. Silver ions interfere with enzymes in the bacteria that transport nutrients, form structures, and synthesise cell walls; these ions also bond with the bacteria's genetic material. Silver and silver nanoparticles are used as an antimicrobial in a variety of industrial, healthcare, and domestic application: for example, infusing clothing with nanosilver particles thus allows them to stay odourless for longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann83_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann83-112">[112]</a></sup> Bacteria can, however, develop resistance to the antimicrobial action of silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panacek-Kvitek-Smekalova-et-al-2018_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panacek-Kvitek-Smekalova-et-al-2018-113">[113]</a></sup> Silver compounds are taken up by the body like <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> compounds, but lack the toxicity of the latter. Silver and its alloys are used in cranial surgery to replace bone, and silver–tin–mercury amalgams are used in dentistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann67_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann67-108">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silver_diammine_fluoride" title="Silver diammine fluoride">Silver diammine fluoride</a>, the fluoride salt of a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complex</a> with the formula [Ag(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>]F, is a topical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicament" title="Medicament">medicament</a> (drug) used to treat and prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_caries" title="Dental caries">dental caries</a> (cavities) and relieve dentinal hypersensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics">Electronics</span></h3>
<p>Silver is very important in electronics for conductors and electrodes on account of its high electrical conductivity even when tarnished. Bulk silver and silver foils were used to make vacuum tubes, and continue to be used today in the manufacture of semiconductor devices, circuits, and their components. For example, silver is used in high quality connectors for <a href="/wiki/Radio_frequency" title="Radio frequency">RF</a>, <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">VHF</a>, and higher frequencies, particularly in tuned circuits such as <a href="/wiki/RF_and_microwave_filter#Cavity_filters" title="RF and microwave filter">cavity filters</a> where conductors cannot be scaled by more than 6%. <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">Printed circuits</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFID" title="RFID">RFID</a> antennas are made with silver paints,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Powdered silver and its alloys are used in paste preparations for conductor layers and electrodes, ceramic capacitors, and other ceramic components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann71_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann71-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazing_alloys">Brazing alloys</span></h3>
<p>Silver-containing <a href="/wiki/Brazing" title="Brazing">brazing</a> alloys are used for brazing metallic materials, mostly <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, and copper-based alloys, tool steels, and precious metals. The basic components are silver and copper, with other elements selected according to the specific application desired: examples include zinc, tin, cadmium, palladium, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>. Silver provides increased workability and corrosion resistance during usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann78_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann78-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_equipment">Chemical equipment</span></h3>
<p>Silver is useful in the manufacture of chemical equipment on account of its low chemical reactivity, high thermal conductivity, and being easily workable. Silver <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucibles</a> (alloyed with 0.15% nickel to avoid recrystallisation of the metal at red heat) are used for carrying out alkaline fusion. Copper and silver are also used when doing chemistry with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>. Equipment made to work at high temperatures is often silver-plated. Silver and its alloys with gold are used as wire or ring seals for oxygen compressors and vacuum equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann81_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann81-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalysis">Catalysis</span></h3>
<p>Silver metal is a good catalyst for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> reactions; in fact it is somewhat too good for most purposes, as finely divided silver tends to result in complete oxidation of organic substances to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water, and hence coarser-grained silver tends to be used instead. For instance, 15% silver supported on α-Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> or silicates is a catalyst for the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">ethylene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_oxide" title="Ethylene oxide">ethylene oxide</a> at 230–270 °C. Dehydrogenation of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> to <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> is conducted at 600–720 °C over silver gauze or crystals as the catalyst, as is dehydrogenation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopropanol" title="Isopropanol">isopropanol</a> to <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>. In the gas phase, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycol" title="Glycol">glycol</a> yields <a href="/wiki/Glyoxal" title="Glyoxal">glyoxal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> yields <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a>, while organic <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a> are dehydrated to <a href="/wiki/Nitrile" title="Nitrile">nitriles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann81_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann81-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photography">Photography</span></h3>
<p>The photosensitivity of the silver halides allowed for their use in traditional photography, although <a href="/wiki/Digital_photography" title="Digital photography">digital photography</a>, which does not use silver, is now dominant. The <a href="/wiki/Photographic_emulsion" title="Photographic emulsion">photosensitive emulsion</a> used in black-and-white photography is a suspension of silver halide crystals in <a href="/wiki/Gelatin" title="Gelatin">gelatin</a>, possibly mixed in with some noble metal compounds for improved photosensitivity, <a href="/wiki/Photographic_processing" title="Photographic processing">developing</a>, and <span class="clarify-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">tuning</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Clarify" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="This passage needs to be better explained. (January 2022)">clarify</span></a></i>]</sup>. <a href="/wiki/Color_photography" title="Color photography">Colour photography</a> requires the addition of special dye components and sensitisers, so that the initial black-and-white silver image couples with a different dye component. The original silver images are bleached off and the silver is then recovered and recycled. Silver nitrate is the starting material in all cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann82_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann82-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of silver nitrate and silver halides in photography has rapidly declined with the advent of digital technology. From the peak global demand for photographic silver in 1999 (267,000,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_ounce" title="Troy ounce">troy ounces</a> or 8,304.6 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>) the market contracted almost 70% by 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-photosdemand_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-photosdemand-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanoparticles">Nanoparticles</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silver_nanoparticle" title="Silver nanoparticle">Silver nanoparticle</a></div>
<p>Nanosilver particles, between 10 and 100 nanometres in size, are used in many applications. They are used in conductive inks for printed electronics, and have a much lower melting point than larger silver particles of micrometre size. They are also used medicinally in antibacterials and antifungals in much the same way as larger silver particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann83_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann83-112">[112]</a></sup> In addition, according to the <a class="external text" href="https://euon.echa.europa.eu/" rel="nofollow">European Union Observatory for Nanomaterials (EUON)</a>, silver nanoparticles are used both in pigments, as well as cosmetics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellanea">Miscellanea</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg/220px-Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg/330px-Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg/440px-Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diwali_sweets_India_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tray of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asian_sweets" title="South Asian sweets">South Asian sweets</a>, with some pieces covered with shiny silver <i>vark</i></div></div></div>
<p>Pure silver metal is used as a food colouring. It has the <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E174</a> designation and is approved in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martínez-AbadOcio2013_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martínez-AbadOcio2013-123">[123]</a></sup> Traditional Indian and Pakistani dishes sometimes include decorative silver foil known as <i><a href="/wiki/Vark" title="Vark">vark</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ss_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ss-124">[124]</a></sup> and in various other cultures, silver <i><a href="/wiki/Drag%C3%A9e" title="Dragée">dragée</a></i> are used to decorate cakes, cookies, and other dessert items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-latimes-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Photochromic_lens" title="Photochromic lens">Photochromic lenses</a> include silver halides, so that ultraviolet light in natural daylight liberates metallic silver, darkening the lenses. The silver halides are reformed in lower light intensities. Colourless silver chloride films are used in <a href="/wiki/Particle_detector" title="Particle detector">radiation detectors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">Zeolite</a> sieves incorporating Ag<sup>+</sup> ions are used to <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalinate</a> seawater during rescues, using silver ions to precipitate chloride as silver chloride. Silver is also used for its antibacterial properties for water sanitisation, but the application of this is limited by limits on silver consumption. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloidal_silver" title="Colloidal silver">Colloidal silver</a> is similarly used to disinfect closed swimming pools; while it has the advantage of not giving off a smell like <a href="/wiki/Hypochlorite" title="Hypochlorite">hypochlorite</a> treatments do, colloidal silver is not effective enough for more contaminated open swimming pools. Small <a href="/wiki/Silver_iodide" title="Silver iodide">silver iodide</a> crystals are used in <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">cloud seeding</a> to cause rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann83_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann83-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Texas_Legislature" title="Texas Legislature">Texas Legislature</a> designated silver the official precious metal of Texas in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1084375498">.mw-parser-output .ib-chembox{border-collapse:collapse;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .ib-chembox td,.mw-parser-output .ib-chembox th{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;width:40%}.mw-parser-output .ib-chembox td+td{width:60%}</style>

</th></tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704"><b>NFPA 704</b></a> (fire diamond)
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="container" style="margin:0 auto; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div class="nounderlines" id="on_image_elements" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" role="img" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_fbee98d65da2341e"><area alt="Health 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g. sodium chloride" coords="23,23,47,47,23,70,0,47" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" shape="poly" title="Health 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g. sodium chloride"/><area alt="Flammability 0: Will not burn. E.g. water" coords="47,0,70,23,47,47,23,23" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" title="Flammability 0: Will not burn. E.g. water"/><area alt="Instability 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g. liquid nitrogen" coords="70,23,94,47,70,70,47,47" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" shape="poly" title="Instability 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g. liquid nitrogen"/><area alt="Special hazards (white): no code" coords="47,47,70,70,47,94,23,70" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" title="Special hazards (white): no code"/></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_fbee98d65da2341e" width="80"/></div></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g. sodium chloride">0</span></a></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability 0: Will not burn. E.g. water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Instability 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g. liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Chemical compound</div>
<p>Silver compounds have low toxicity compared to those of most other <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, as they are poorly absorbed by the human body when ingested, and that which does get absorbed is rapidly converted to insoluble silver compounds or complexed by <a href="/wiki/Metallothionein" title="Metallothionein">metallothionein</a>. However, silver fluoride and silver nitrate are caustic and can cause tissue damage, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarrhoea" title="Diarrhoea">diarrhoea</a>, falling <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>, cramps, paralysis, and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_arrest" title="Respiratory arrest">respiratory arrest</a>. Animals repeatedly dosed with silver salts have been observed to experience <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaemia" title="Anaemia">anaemia</a>, slowed growth, <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a> of the liver, and fatty degeneration of the liver and kidneys; rats implanted with silver foil or injected with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloidal_silver" title="Colloidal silver">colloidal silver</a> have been observed to develop localised tumours. <a href="/wiki/Route_of_administration" title="Route of administration">Parenterally</a> admistered colloidal silver causes acute silver poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann88_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann88-128">[128]</a></sup> Some waterborne species are particularly sensitive to silver salts and those of the other precious metals; in most situations, however, silver does not pose serious environmental hazards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann88_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann88-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>In large doses, silver and compounds containing it can be absorbed into the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> and become deposited in various body tissues, leading to <a href="/wiki/Argyria" title="Argyria">argyria</a>, which results in a blue-grayish pigmentation of the skin, eyes, and <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a>. Argyria is rare, and so far as is known, does not otherwise harm a person's health, though it is disfiguring and usually permanent. Mild forms of argyria are sometimes mistaken for <a href="/wiki/Cyanosis" title="Cyanosis">cyanosis</a>, a blue tint on skin, caused by lack of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann88_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann88-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Metallic silver, like copper, is an antibacterial agent, which was known to the ancients and first scientifically investigated and named the <a href="/wiki/Oligodynamic_effect" title="Oligodynamic effect">oligodynamic effect</a> by <a href="/wiki/Carl_N%C3%A4geli" title="Carl Nägeli">Carl Nägeli</a>. Silver ions damage the metabolism of bacteria even at such low concentrations as 0.01–0.1 milligrams per litre; metallic silver has a similar effect due to the formation of silver oxide. This effect is lost in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> due to the extreme insolubility of silver sulfide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann88_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann88-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some silver compounds are very explosive, such as the nitrogen compounds silver azide, silver <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide</a>, and silver fulminate, as well as <a href="/wiki/Silver_acetylide" title="Silver acetylide">silver acetylide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_oxalate" title="Silver oxalate">silver oxalate</a>, and silver(II) oxide. They can explode on heating, force, drying, illumination, or sometimes spontaneously. To avoid the formation of such compounds, ammonia and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> should be kept away from silver equipment. Salts of silver with strongly oxidising acids such as <a href="/wiki/Silver_chlorate" title="Silver chlorate">silver chlorate</a> and silver nitrate can explode on contact with materials that can be readily oxidised, such as organic compounds, sulfur and soot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann88_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann88-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
